13. ABSTRACT (Maximum 200 words)
This documents reports a cultural resource inventory conducted on 12 separate tracts of land, totaling
1,810 acres, in seven counties in East Texas, including Angelina, Bowie, Jasper, Marion, Morris, San
Augustine, and Upshur Counties. The inventory area includes 364 acres in three parcels at Lake O’ the
Pines, 546 acres in three parcels at Lake Wright Patman, 711 acres in four parcels at Lake Sam Rayburn,
178 acres at Steinhagen Lake, and 11 acres at White Oak Creek Mitigation Area (WOCMA). Archeological
fieldwork was conducted between April 17 and May 11, 2002 and included 100% pedestrian inventory
accompanied by more than 2,000 shovel tests. Eleven archeological sites were documented, including
three previously recorded sites, seven newly discovered sites, and one historic site whose location was
known but which had never been recorded. In addition, the locations of two other previously recorded sites
were investigated but could not be verified. Of the 13 sites, one will not be affected by the proposed
undertaking, and five sites are recommended as not eligible for inclusion to the National Register of
Historic Places (NRHP). Using the inventory tactics, it could not be determined whether or not the
remaining seven sites may be eligible to the NRHP. All should be protected from adverse impacts; if they
can not be protected, they should be further investigated to clearly determine NRHP eligibility.

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

ABSTRACT
This report documents the substantive findings and management recommendations of a cultural
resource inventory conducted on 12 separate tracts of land, totaling 1,810 acres, in seven
counties in East Texas, including Angelina, Bowie, Jasper, Marion, Morris, San Augustine, and
Upshur Counties. The inventory area includes 364 acres in three parcels at Lake O’ the Pines,
546 acres in three parcels at Lake Wright Patman, 711 acres in four parcels at Lake Sam
Rayburn, 178 acres at Steinhagen Lake, and 11 acres at White Oak Creek Mitigation Area
(WOCMA). Work was conducted at the request of the United States Army Corps of Engineers,
Fort Worth District (USACE). The USACE has proposed timber harvesting within 11 of the 12
selected parcels and, in compliance with Section 106 of the National Historic Preservation Act
(NHPA), an archeological inventory was undertaken to ensure that any cultural resource sites
located on the parcels would be considered in planning and conducting the undertaking. Work at
the twelfth parcel, the WOCMA tract, was done to better define the boundaries of two previously
known sites.
Archeological fieldwork was conducted between April 17 and May 11, 2002. Fieldwork
included a 100% pedestrian inventory accompanied by intensive and systematic shovel testing.
More than 2,000 shovel tests were manually excavated, resulting in a net density of 1.1 tests per
acre. Eleven archeological sites were documented, including three previously recorded sites,
seven newly discovered sites, and one historic site whose location was known but which had
never been recorded. In addition, the locations of two other previously recorded sites were
investigated but could not be verified.
Of the 13 sites, one will not be affected by the proposed undertaking, and five sites are
recommended as not eligible for inclusion to the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP).
Eligibility of the remaining seven sites is undetermined.
•

Sites 41MR108 is not within the current APE and will not be affected by the proposed
undertaking.

•

Sites, 41AG183, 41AG184, 41BW252, 41BW691, and 41JP151 have low potential for
further research and are recommended as not eligible for inclusion to the NRHP. No further
management is warranted for these sites.

•

A clear assessment of eligibility could not be achieved for sites 41JP152, 41JP153, 41BW76,
41BW690, 41AG182, 41MX29, and 41MX30. Using the limited shovel testing inventory
tactics, it is unknown whether or not any of these sites may be eligible for inclusion to the
NRHP. All should be protected from adverse impacts. If they can not be protected, they
should be further investigated to clearly determine NRHP eligibility.

1.0 PROJECT DESCRIPTION
1.1 THE UNDERTAKING
The United States Army Corps of Engineers, Fort Worth District (USACE) is responsible for
managing certain federally-owned lands in East Texas in association with lakes, reservoirs and
navigable waterways. These management responsibilities include 9,251 acres at Lake O’ Pines,
17,461 acres at Lake Sam Rayburn, 7,943 acres at Steinhagen Lake, and 45,063 acres at Lake
Wright Patman (acreages are outside conservation pools). Much of this acreage is forested by
mature pine and pine-hardwood stands. In conjunction with the existing management plans for
the several reservoirs and in accordance with accepted silviculture methodologies, the USACE
has planned certain management areas for selective timber thinning. Commercial thinning
removes marketable trees in uneven-aged stands (not clear-cut) through single tree selection.
Removal of crop trees often disturbs the ground surface but extensive subsurface disturbance is
not common.
While planning and conducting undertakings that have the potential to affect historic properties,
the USACE must take such resources into account, and in compliance with Section 106 of the
National Historic Preservation Act (NHPA) of 1966 as amended through 1992 (P.L. 89-665 et
seq.), the inventory was authorized by the USACE to identify any archeological sites, buildings,
structures, or other resources that may be eligible for inclusion to the National Register of
Historic Places (NRHP). All inventories were conducted on lands owned by the Federal
Government and managed by the USACE with the exception of the 11 acres at the White Oak
Creek Mitigation Area (WOCMA), which are fee owned by the USACE and managed under a
license to the State of Texas, Texas Parks and Wildlife Division (TPWD).
1.2 LOCATION
The project area is comprised of 12 separate parcels in seven counties. Eleven of the parcels are
located at four different East Texas lakes. The twelfth parcel is located at the WOCMA in East
Texas (Figure 1-1). The total area inventoried is 1,810 acres (Table 1-1).
1.2.1 Lake O’ the Pines
Lake O’ the Pines is located on Big Cypress Creek 14 km west of Jefferson and approximately
40 km northeast of Longview in Marion, Harrison, Morris, Upshur, and Camp counties. The
earthen dam was constructed between 1955 and 1959 and impounds a reservoir with 18,700
surface acres. The lake is used for flood control, recreation, and water and wildlife conservation
(Breeding 2002a). Three tracts totaling 364 acres are currently planned for timber thinning
(Figure 1-2).
•

The Highway 259 Area is located in Upshur County at the extreme northwestern (upstream)
point of Lake O’ the Pines, about 4 km south of the community of Lonestar and about 10 km
north of Ore City (see Figure A-1). Totaling 81 acres, this parcel is situated immediately
south of a bend of Highway 259 the Angelina River and west of Cypress Creek. The tract

•

The Ferrell’s Dam Area is located in Marion County immediately beneath the Lake O’ the
Pines impoundment structure and south of Highway 726 (Figure A-3). The area consists of
three separate parcels totaling 205 acres. Area “A” is a 32-acre parcel measuring about 400
m by 200 m. It is connected to Area “B” by a thin strip paralleling the structure frontage
road. Area “B” is 78 acres in size with dimensions of about 300 m by 1.9 km. Area “C” is
located south and east of the structure spillway. It is 95 acres and measures about 700 m by
700 m in maximum dimensions. Because of their proximity, these three parcels were treated
as a single sampling unit. Areas “A” and “B” are located on the Harleton USGS 7.5’
topographic quadrangle and Area “C” is located on the Marshall NW and the Kellyville
USGS 7.5’ topographic quadrangles. Elevation ranges from about 200 ft to 300 ft AMSL.

1.2.2 Lake Wright Patman
Lake Wright Patman is on the Sulphur River 18 km southwest of Texarkana in Bowie and Cass
Counties. The far upstream portions of the reservoir extend into Morris, Titus, and Red River
Counties. Initially known as Texarkana Reservoir, the lake was renamed in the late 1970s in
honor of the East Texas congressman (Breeding 2002b). The dam was constructed between
1948 and 1957 and impounds an area of 20,300 acres. Conservation pool elevation is 220 ft
AMSL. Four parcels totaling 546 acres are currently planned for timber thinning (Figure 1-3).
•

1.2.3 Lake Sam Rayburn
Lake Sam Rayburn is located on the Angelina River approximately 15 miles north of Jasper,
Texas. Authorized by Congress in 1955 for the purposes of flood control, hydroelectric power
generation, and conservation of water for municipalities, industry, agriculture, and recreation,
construction of the reservoir was begun by the USACE in 1956 and impoundment began in 1965.
Initially known as “McGee Bend Dam and Reservoir,” the lake was renamed in honor of Speaker
of the U.S. House of Representatives Sam Rayburn (USACE 2002b). The lake is the largest
body of water within the boundaries of the State of Texas. At conservation pool level (164.4 ft
AMSL) the lake has approximately 750 miles of shoreline (USACE 2002b). Approximately
17,461 acres of fee lands is managed by the USACE. Four tracts totaling 711 acres are currently
planned for timber thinning (Figure 1-4).
•

The Sunset Ridge Area is located in Jasper County just north of the Sam Rayburn control
structure. Totaling 46 acres, this parcel consists of a narrow strip of shoreline that measures
about 50 to 100 m in width along the winding shoreline (see Figure A-9, northeast corner).
The tract is located on the McGee Bend USGS 7.5’ topographic quadrangle. Adjoining the
upslope boundary of the U.S. Government property line is the private subdivision of Rayburn
Country, consisting of lake-view homes.

•

The Cove Area is also located in Jasper County just north of the Sam Rayburn control
structure. Totaling 94 acres, this tract is similarly consists of a narrow strip of shoreline that
measures about 50 to 100 m in width along the winding shoreline (Figure A-9, southwest
corner). The tract is located on the McGee Bend USGS 7.5’ topographic quadrangle.
Adjoining the upslope boundary of the U.S. Government property line is the private
subdivision of Rayburn Country, consisting of lake-view homes.

1.2.4 Steinhagen Lake
Lake B. A. Steinhagen is situated on the Neches River about 17 km southwest of Jasper in Tyler
and Jasper counties. Impounded by the Town Bluff Dam, the lake is about 8 miles long and 4
miles wide (Figure 1-5). Martin Dies, Jr. State Park and Sandy Creek Park are on its banks.
Construction was started in March 1947, deliberate
impoundment began April 1951, and the dam was
Spring Creek
East Area
completed in June 1953. The dam is operated by the
USACE (USACE 2002c).

quadrangles. Totaling 178 acres, this tract is

1.2.5 White Oak Creek Mitigation Area

Figure 1-5 Inventory Area at Lake
Steinhagen.

The White Oak Creek Mitigation Area consists of 25,700 acres northeast of Mount Pleasant, in
Bowie, Cass, Morris, and Titus Counties. This is the only property in the current inventory
project that is not associated with a USACE lake (Figure 1-6). Although owned by the USACE,
the WOCMA is managed by the TPWD and provides for public hunting of white-tailed deer,
feral hog, dove, quail, woodcock, snipe, waterfowl, rabbits, hares, and squirrel. The property is
managed under a license agreement with the USACE (TPWD 2002).
•

Sites 41MX29 and 41MX30. The inventory sample consists of an 11 acre parcel in Morris
County north of the TPWD offices on Highway 77, near Naples Texas. The tract is located

1.3 NATURAL ENVIRONMENT
The project is located in the Piney Woods region of deep East Texas. Forested areas in the
region included secondary growth and mature pine and pine-hardwood stands (Figures 1-7 and
1-8). Pine stands observed during the field inventory were typically comprised of loblolly (Pinus
taeda), shortleaf (Pinus echinata), and longleaf (Pinus palustris) pines. Hardwood trees were
predominantly various oaks, including water oak (Quercus nigra), southern red oak (Quercus
falcate), and white oak (Quercus alba). Other representative hardwoods present in the area
include American sycamore (Platanus occidentalis), sweetgum (Liquidambar styraciflua), bald
cypress (Taxodium spp.), and Dahoon holly (Ilex cassine).
Wildlife common to the area included whitetail deer, red fox, bobcat, tree squirrels, raccoons and
opossums, armadillo, nutria, otters, and mink. Sam Rayburn is home to a variety of turtles, frogs
and snakes, including the water moccasin (cottonmouth) and the copperhead. The American

Figure 1-7 Mixed PineHardwood Forest.
Ecological Communications Corporation

Figure 1-8 Mixed Pine-Hardwood
Forest.

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

alligator is a recently introduced species. Bird species include the endangered red-cockaded
woodpecker, as well as bald eagles, osprey, bobwhite and mourning dove, white pelicans,
common loons, herons, and egrets. Fish species include largemouth bass, flathead catfish,
channel catfish, white and black crappie, white bass, and bluegill and other sunfish (USACE
2002b).
Much of the area inventoried shows evidence of some degree of previous disturbance; the area
has been previously logged and/or been commercially thinned. Also, due to the construction of
the artificial reservoirs, some terrain was swampy and a high water table was observed in many
shovel tests. Some areas were obviously eroded. Many individual shovel tests recorded soil
mottling, gravels or other evidence of subsurface disturbance (Figures 1-9 and 1-10).

2.0 PREVIOUS RESEARCH
The history and prehistory of East Texas have been studied through numerous historical and
archeological investigations. Many small investigations have been conducted by the Texas
Department of Transportation (TxDOT), on rural developments by the Federal Highway
Administration (FHWA), and various projects for utilities and mineral interests. Of these small
projects, the inventories crossing lowland areas have generally recorded higher site densities than
the upland inventories. Larger investigations have been focused on the several reservoirs
(Perttula et al. 1998) and on the Big Thicket National Preserve (Shafer et al. 1975, Brown et al.
1999). Large investigations in the region generally show that both prehistoric and historic sites
were found adjacent to the floodplains on first terraces or along the toe slopes of higher ridges.
In general, areas around stream confluences and on small rises within floodplains had a good to
excellent chance of containing prehistoric cultural resources while historic resources can be in
more diverse conditions, including areas away from water.
2.1 PREVIOUSLY RECORDED SITES
A map and file search was conducted at the archives of the Texas Archeological Research
Laboratory (TARL) on March 19 and March 22, 2002 and a map and file search was
subsequently conducted at the Texas Historical Commission (THC). These searches determined
that five previously recorded archeological sites are plotted within the boundaries of the several
selected inventory parcels. One site is located within the Ferrell’s Dam area at Lake O’ the
Pines, two sites are located within the two Highway 59 areas at Lake Wright Patman, and two
sites are located within the White Oak Creek Mitigation Area (Table 2-1). In addition, the USGS
topographic map for the Marion’s Ferry area at Lake Sam Rayburn shows a map symbol labeled
“Site of Marion;” this known historic location was not previously recorded as an archeological
site. In addition, at least 130 sites have been recorded within one-mile radii of the several
parcels.

2.2 CULTURAL HISTORY FRAMEWORK
Humans have lived in East Texas counties for at least 11,000 years. The currently accepted
framework of culture history is outlined by Perttula (1993) in a planning document for East
Texas. This region identifies populations ranging from the Paleo-Indian (prior to 7000 B.C.) to
Archaic foraging cultures (ca. 7000-100 B.C.) to Late Cultures (A.D. 100-1700) (Story 1990).
These time periods are based on changes in environment, technology, and/or population
requiring cultural adaptations to meet varying circumstances.
2.2.1 The Paleo-Indian Period
The earliest known time of human occupation in North America is called the Paleo-Indian
Period. The archeological evidence suggests that during this time the earliest residents of eastern
Texas were highly nomadic hunters and gatherers. Populations were small and dispersed and
exploited a variety of wild food resources (Story 1990; Bryant and Shafer 1977:19-20). The
economy was based on a lithic technology. Large leaf-shaped projectile points, such as the
widespread Folsom and Clovis, are diagnostic of this time period and suggest that mega-fauna
such as extinct bison were key resources.
Paleo-Indian sites are rare and often consist of isolated finds of diagnostic artifacts. Artifacts
recovered in the region suggest that early occupations were principally distributed on Tertiary
deposits in the valleys of major stream basins (Perttula 1995). These finds were typically
lanceolate projectile points with ground, concave bases and longitudinal fluting. Toward the end
of the Paleo-Indian Period, point styles gradually changed to more stemmed and barbed varieties.
A small number of archeological sites dating to this earliest time period are known to occur in
the region. In the Northeast and Southeast Texas regions, only 53 archeological sites have been
dated to the Paleo-Indian Period (Biesaart 1985:86-90), or less than 2% of the total sites recorded
(n = 4,026). As a consequence of this scarcity, these inherently valuable archeological sites are
usually considered to be significant cultural resources.
2.2.2 The Archaic Period
Near the end of the Paleo-Indian Period, global climate began to change slowly, becoming
gradually warmer and drier (Story 1990). In response, plant and animal populations also
changed and the human populations in East Texas began to exploit a wider variety of food
resources. Large game was no longer the primary focus of subsistence. Changes in technology
included a more diverse suite of lithic tools, increased use of grinding stones and the
development of basketry. Pottery is absent. This adaptation is known as the Archaic Period and
has been dated in East Texas as occurring between about 7000 and 100 years B.C. During this
period, hunter-gatherers retained a hunting-gathering subsistence economy but also developed
more regionally specialized approaches toward exploiting their environment (Story 1990).
The Archaic Period is subdivided into Early, Middle, and Late divisions. Each of these
subdivisions has characteristic types of artifacts, especially projectile points, which suggest
differences in economy and possibly regional affiliations. The Early Archaic is viewed as

Page 10

Ecological Communications Corporation

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

transitional from Paleo-Indian to Archaic (Duffield 1963) with technology and population as
driving forces, requiring a greater reliance on the inferior, local East Texas lithic resources and a
greater emphasis on territoriality. The Middle Archaic subsistence strategies remained
dependent on a hunting and gathering economy with a greater reliance on native plants
(Thurmond 1990). The Late Archaic period occupations remained dependent on seasonal
variations in food sources and probably moved whenever a food source became scarce or
unavailable. Later Archaic site distributions show a shift from major streams to minor streams
and natural springs (Perttula and Skiles 1986:48-53). This greater number and distribution of
Late Archaic sites in East Texas suggests the successful adaptation to the environment lasting
until the development of pottery (around 500-100 B.C.) and/or until the development of the bow
and arrow.
Of the more than 4,000 prehistoric archeological sites recorded in the Northeast and Southeast
regions of Texas, about 31% date to the Archaic Period (Biesaart et al. 1985:86-90). Archaic
Period archeological sites are thus not quite as rare as are Paleo-Indian sites, but their data
potential can be significant, especially if they are undisturbed. As a consequence, a high
proportion of Archaic Period sites are considered to be significant cultural resources.
2.2.3 Late Cultures
Following the Archaic Period, Late Cultures continued the foraging-gathering subsistence
strategy but also developed new technologies, especially acquiring game with the bow and arrow
and preparing and storing food using ceramic vessels. In the inland southeastern area of Texas,
this population is commonly referred to as the Mossy Grove culture (Story 1990). Although
better identified in the Galveston Bay or Lake Livingston areas, several sites as far north as the
Neches River in Angelina County have characteristics of this culture. The Mossy Grove culture
is generally distinguished by Goose Creek Plain ceramics, a plain sandy paste ware described by
Aten (1983). It is thought that most Mossy Grove groups were hunters and gatherers and
practiced little or no agriculture. Most are believed to have practiced a seasonal-round
subsistence pattern with no permanent settlement. Although the Mossy Grove culture continued
until historic times in the Galveston Bay and Lake Livingston areas, it along with other
Woodland cultures was replaced by the Caddoan culture in the project vicinity at approximately
A.D. 900 (Story 1990).
The Caddoan culture (A.D. 900-1700) was an indigenous development strongly influenced by
the Mississippian tradition of the Lower Mississippi Valley (Wyckoff 1971; Story 1990:323).
Caddo subsistence and social organization differ markedly from earlier periods. Horticulture and
then agriculture supplemented hunting and gathering. Larger aggregates of people became
sedentary and constructed villages with public ceremonial areas. Cemeteries are found in
association with large ceremonial mounds.
A stratified social structure developed,
corresponding to the chiefdom level as first defined by Service (1962; 1975). Extensive
commercial networks were also established. Larger settlements were mainly distributed on
elevated landforms adjacent to major streams, while smaller farmsteads were found along minor
tributaries and spring-fed branches. Hamlets and farmsteads were the most common type of
Caddoan settlement, although larger communities occurred in association with mound centers.
The Caddo tradition lasted until European domination of the area.

Ecological Communications Corporation

Page 11

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

Although the Late Cultures flourished in East Texas for only about 1,800 years or 15% of the
length of human occupation, sites dating to this period are most common. Of the more than
4,000 prehistoric archeological sites recorded in the northeast and southeast regions of Texas,
nearly 60% date to this period (Biesaart et al. 1985:86-90). Although more common than
Archaic sites, preservation of buried materials is often better, and the data potential of these sites
can be significant, especially if they are undisturbed.
2.2.4 Historic Period
The inhabitants of East Texas were first contacted by Europeans between 1528 and 1533 by the
Spanish Explorer, Cabeza de Vaca. This initial foray is believed to have encountered the
Caddoan Adai somewhere between Nacogdoches, Texas and Nachitoches, Louisiana. It was
followed by the explorer Moscoso, whose report of crossing the Angelina River mentions several
distinct Caddoan groups including the Ayish, Nondacoa, Nasoni, Nacacahoz, and Naquisca. The
French Explorer La Salle in 1685 also reported Hasinai Caddo near Nacogdoches and in 1690
the Spanish began establishing a series of missions in the area, including two at Nacogdoches by
1716. The Spaniards reported that the local people actively cultivated fields of maize, which
they supplemented with gardens of beans, squash, and other vegetables, as well as hunting and
fishing. Trotlining was a common fishing technique. Surplus maize was often traded with
neighboring people (USACE 2002b).
The Historic Period (1700-present) not only describes the history behind the current cultural
setting of the area but also marks a transition from the native populations to the European
immigrant’s establishment of farms, towns, and counties. With the arrival of European settlers,
Historic Indian populations were introduced to immigrants from other tribes and eventual
disposition to Indian Territory outside of Texas. As the indigenous groups were exposed to
Europeans, trade items such as glass beads, metal points, buttons, and gun parts made their way
into the archaeological record. Unfortunately, contact with the Europeans also brought new
diseases, which severely reduced the populations of the Caddoan Confederacy villages.
Remnants of these once-numerous people now reside in Oklahoma and Louisiana (USACE
2002b).
Area of Lake Wright Patman - Bowie, Cass, Morris Counties
The first likely European expedition into the area was led by Luis de Moscoso Alvarado in 1542.
Moscoso was a member of the Hernando de Soto’s expedition into the southeastern U.S. and
took over after he died in 1539. Looking for an overland route back to New Spain, he crossed
into the territory that became Bowie County and used existing trails established by the Caddo to
move further into the interior of Texas before turning around and traveling back to the coast
(Bruseth and Kenmotsu 1993). French visitors to the area included Henri Jountel, who was part
of the LaSalle expedition, and Henri de Tonti, who went to the area to look for survivors from
Fort St. Louis (Harper 2002b). Permanent European presence began in 1719, when Le Poste des
Cadodaquious was established by the French at a location just to the northwest of Texarkana.
Although a fort in name, the establishment served more as a trading station and supply base for
further expeditions. The fort was abandoned a few years after the Louisiana cession to Spain in

Page 12

Ecological Communications Corporation

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

1762. The fort was reestablished in 1770 by the Spanish, under the name San Luiz de los
Cadodachos, but the Nasoni tribes they were trying to foster a relationship with left the area soon
after (Britton 2002). Finally, the Americans explored the area in 1806 when the Red River
Expedition launched by Thomas Jefferson traveled up the Red River to Spanish Bluff, where
they were forced to turn back by Spanish soldiers.
Immigration and settlement of the areas which would become Bowie and Cass Counties began in
the 1820s and 1830s. Both Anglo-Americans and members of the Shawnee, Delaware, and
Kickapoo Indians settled there, but the Native-American tribes moved on within ten years. By
1846, the establishment of separate counties had been made and county lines had been resolved.
The white settlers were primarily from the South, and set up slave-operated cotton plantations as
well as raising corn, hogs, and some cattle. Just before the Civil War, the slave population
outnumbered the white population in Bowie County, and numbered just under half in Cass
County (Harper 2002a; Harper 2002b). As was expected, the white population strongly
supported the secession movement and the Confederate war effort. Although no battle took
place in the area, federal troops occupied Bowie County in 1867 on behalf of the Freedmen’s
Bureau to assist black residents in obtaining their legal and political equality. This enflamed
white residents, who had lost much of their capital once the slaves were freed, and secretly
supported anti-black movements such as Cullen Baker’s gang and the Ku Klux Klan.
By the 1870s, the Texas and Pacific Railway was being built and several towns sprouted up in
the area. Texarkana was founded in 1873, at the site of an abandoned Caddo village, where two
major railroads would soon meet (Chandler and Howe 1939). It immediately became the largest
trading and manufacturing center in the area. Maud was formed in 1870. Atlanta was founded
in 1871, and it, along with Douglassville, was an economic center for the surrounding rural
community (Hazlewood 2002; Harper 2002c).
The entire area remained primarily rural with an agricultural economy, but small manufacturing
also employed some of the local population. The Depression had a devastating effect on the
area, but World War II brought the establishment of the Red River Army Depot and the Lone
Star Army Ammunition Plant, which increasingly employed many of the local residents from
that point forward (Harper 2002a). Changes in the economic market drew many people away
from farming and into manufacturing and trade. However, populations grew slowly, if at all, and
the inhabitants remained relatively poor. Wright Patman Lake was completed in 1957, and
provided recreation facilities as well as flood control measures.
Area of Lake O’ the Pines – Marion, Harrison, Upshur, Camp Counties
Much of the area around Big Cypress Creek and what would become Lake O’ the Pines
remained uninhabited for most of the eighteenth century. The first Anglo-American settlements
were formed in the 1840s, and the town of Jefferson was founded at that time. The settlers were
primarily from Tennessee and Alabama; the area of east Upshur County and Marion County
were inhabited by those from Alabama. They set up a way of life similar to what they had at
home, which included large cotton plantations using slave labor (Atkins 2002; Kirby 2002).
Jefferson quickly became a booming river port town, in fact the largest inland port in Texas.
North Texas agricultural produce was shipped to Shreveport and New Orleans, and as a result of

Ecological Communications Corporation

Page 13

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

this connection with the Mississippi River system and factories to the east, new amenities such as
refrigeration, soda water, artificial gas for lighting, and ice production made their first
appearance as early as the 1870s (Atkins 2002). The white population supported the secession
movement and the Confederacy in the Civil War, and ordinance, rifles, and tinned food were
manufactured for the Confederate forces. Military occupation followed the Civil War, although
white suppression of the black majority continued to persist. Jefferson declined significantly in
1873 when the destruction of the “raft” – a massive log jam - on the Red River above Shreveport
reduced the water levels of Big Cypress Creek, as well as the establishment of rail transportation
in other parts of the state (Long 2002a).
Rich iron ore deposits in eastern Upshur County were mined on and off since the 1860s, but the
town of Ore City was not founded until 1911, anticipating the imminent arrival of a rail line to
transport the ore to smelters. Ore City operated as a mining and lumbering center, and managed
to survive after these major industries became non-operational (Long 2002b). Oil was
discovered in the 1910s, and gave a boost to the manufacturing sector. The Depression dealt the
area a hard blow, especially the agricultural economy, but rising oil prices and federal assistance
helped the area recover more quickly than other areas of Texas. The economy and population of
the area declined slightly over the next 30 years, but the area moved more toward a tourist
economy with the development of urban heritage sites in Jefferson, obtaining NRHP status in
1971, as well as water recreation from Caddo Lake State Park and Lake O’ the Pines, which
were developed in 1930s and 1950s, respectively.
Area of Lake Sam Rayburn – San Augustine, Jasper, Angelina, Nacodoches Counties
The first European eyes to see this part of the country were likely part of the Luis de Moscoso
Alvarado expedition, who may have traveled through the area on his return journey through
Texas in 1542 (Long 2002c). Later on, French traders were traveling through the area and traded
with the native inhabitants in the 1690s (McCroskey 2002). This prompted the Spanish to pay
more attention to the region, and in 1691 Domingo Terán de los Ríos cut a path that would
eventually become the Old San Antonio Road. However, settlement of the area by the Spanish
did not begin until 1716 and 1717, when Domingo Ramón and Father Antonio Margil de Jesús
founded Mission Nuestra Señora de los Dolores de los Ais near modern San Augustine, Mission
Nuestra Señora de la Purísma Concepción de los Hainai near modern Douglass, Mission San
José de los Nazonis near modern Cushing, and Mission Nuestra Señora de Guadalupe de los
Nacogdoches in modern Nacogdoches (Burke 1971; Mason 1974). Presidio Nuestra Señora de
los Dolores de los Tejas was also established at the time. Most of the area was abandoned in
1719 due to fighting with the French, but the Spanish returned in 1720. Soon after two trails
linked the area to the rest of Spanish Texas: the Old San Antonio Road or Camino Real,
connected the San Antonio area to Nacogdoches, and the Liberty-Nacogdoches Road headed
south from Nacogdoches to El Orcoquisac near the mouth of the Trinity River. Missions
Purísma Concepción and San José de los Nazonis were eventually moved to the San Antonio
area in 1730 and 1731, but the other missions and presidio remained in the area until the cession
of the Louisiana territory to Spain in 1763 negated the necessity of their continued presence that
far east. The missions and presidio were abandoned in 1773.

Page 14

Ecological Communications Corporation

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

The area around the future Lake Sam Rayburn was re-inhabited by Antonio Gil Ibarvo and his
fellow settlers in 1779, at the location of the previously abandoned mission in the Nacogdoches
area. Areas to the southeast of Nacogdoches along the Angelina River, called the Ayish Bayou
District, were also settled by the Spanish in the late eighteenth century. Shortly after
Nacogdoches’ founding, Ibarvo built a building called the “Old Stone Fort” for business and
administration, and the settlement became a center for illegal trade between Spanish, French, and
Americans (Long 2002c; McDonald 1981). Throughout the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries
Nacogdoches was the central location of politics in East Texas. The town was sacked by Joaquín
de Arredondo around 1812 for housing supporters of Augustus Magee and Bernardo Guitiérrez,
it became the stage for a battle in 1832 that helped instigate the Texas Revolution, as well as the
center of the Córdova uprising 1838 (McDonald 1980).
San Augustine, the only other densely inhabited area during this time period, also participated
heavily in political activities and conflicts (Crocket 1932). Although the region was constantly
contested in the 1820s and 1830s, Anglo-American settlers continued to come, and set up farms
in what would become Nacogdoches, Angelina, San Augustine, Tyler, and Jasper Counties.
Bevil’s Settlement, later called Jasper, was founded in 1824 by John Bevil, and became the
county seat of Jasper County in 1844 (Marin 1953; Smith 1979). The first ferry and port town in
the area, Town Bluff, was settled on the Neches River in 1833 as part of the Lorenzo de Zavala’s
colony (Biesele 2002a). Woodville was established in 1846 when Tyler became a county, and
quickly established itself as the county seat (Biesele 2002b).
By 1844, after the Regulator-Moderator War in the east, most of the area had seen the last of
openly violent conflict (McCroskey 2002). However, a new conflict was brewing that eventually
erupted into the Civil War. The region was settled mostly by Southerners who carried the slaverun plantation culture with them, but unlike their neighbors to the north, much of the
environment was not conducive to cotton production, so many who remained in the area were
poor farmers with few or no slaves, relying on subsistence agriculture, corn, and hogs, as well as
some tobacco, rice, and cattle. Steamboats ferried corn, cotton, and other products down the
Angelina and other navigable rivers to markets in the east, and many towns derived their income
from port fees. As a result, the reaction of the region’s inhabitants to secession was mixed. The
Mexican population in Nacogdoches did not approve of secession, but the area eventually voted
to support the cause (Long 2002c). Angelina County was one of the few counties in the state to
reject secession altogether (Boon 1937). The other counties to the south and east stuck to their
Southern roots and supported the action, sometimes by an overwhelming margin. Once the Civil
War began however, all the inhabitants of the region supported the Confederate Army, supplying
troops and some iron goods. San Augustine was occupied by Confederate troops to guard
against invasion, but no battles were fought in the area.
Nacogdoches, Woodville, and Jasper were occupied by Union troops during Reconstruction, and
some KKK-like organizations were formed to force African-American activists out of the area,
but these organizations dissolved soon after they were created. The region suffered
economically after the Civil War, and it did not begin to recover until the railroads were built in
the 1880s. All but San Augustine County had railroads built by the end of the nineteenth
century, and this mode of transportation enabled lumbering to be developed in full force. Towns
such as Colmesneil, Rockland, Browndell, Zavilla, and Broaddus sprung up around new

Ecological Communications Corporation

Page 15

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

sawmills, and existing towns began to harvest nearby timber for a new source of income (Biesele
2002c; 2002d; St. Clair and St. Clair 1982; Odintz 2002; Wooster 2002). However, poor timber
renewal practices and the Depression caused timber sales to plummet in the 1930s, and several
small towns were deserted. In most areas livestock raising began to replace what little cotton
production was present, and modest oil extraction was developed to support the economy in
Nacogdoches and Jasper Counties. However, the primary economy remained agricultural, and
the people maintained a steady rural lifestyle. Timber harvesting was revived during and after
World War II, but not to the extent of previous years. Other areas began planting sugar cane,
raising poultry, and manufacturing tung (to make lacquer). Lake Sam Rayburn and Steinhagen
Lake were completed in the 1950s and 1960s, and tourism helped to revive the regional
economy.

Ecological Communications Corporation

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

3.0 FIELD METHODS
Archeological inventory was conducted in two field sessions totaling 110 person-days. The first
session was from April 17 through April 26, 2002 and included four field personnel. The second
session was from April 30 through May 11, 2002 and included six field personnel. The Principal
Investigator was in the field for 30% of the time and each session was 100% directly supervised
by the project Field Director, Victor Galan of Nacodoches, Texas. Methods and density of
shovel testing complied with, or exceeded, the minimum requirements for field tactics stipulated
by the USACE (2002a:10-44).
3.1 INVENTORY TACTICS
For each parcel, a logistical plan was prepared in advance by the Field Director who drew
proposed transect lines at 30 m intervals on the topographic map. Each field archeologist was
given a copy of the topographic map and was assigned a transect line. The location of each
shovel test was marked by the field archeologist on the field map. In most cases, transect lines
were designated with an alpha prefix (either arbitrary or using the crew persons initials) and
individual shovel tests along each transect line were numbered. For example, shovel test G-5
would be the fifth test along the seventh transect and shovel test CK-17 designated the
seventeenth test in the parcel dug by Chris Kugler. In general, each crew member followed their
own transect. Occasionally, for logistical reasons, two crew members would “leapfrog” along
one transect, or would double up with one person digging the shovel tests and another screening
and recording the field forms. Where possible, tests were selectively placed on landforms shown
by previous research to be preferred site locations, such as ridge lines, knolls, and promontories.
Where ever shovel tests could not be dug due to standing water, erosion, or steep slopes, these
locations were individually recorded on the shovel testing log.

All excavated artifacts were recovered and were processed and analyzed by the authors in
EComm’s Austin laboratory. All artifacts, notes and records will be curated at TARL, at the
University of Texas, Austin, upon completion of analysis.
3.2 DENSITY OF SHOVEL TESTING
The contractual scope of work issued by the USACE specified that at a minimum, one shovel
test was to be excavated per three acres (equivalent to 0.3 tests per acre).1 Using this rate of
coverage, a total of 637 shovel tests was the minimum required to inventory the 1,810 acres
(Table 3-1). In an attempt to ensure greater coverage than this minimum, a total of 2,041 shovel
tests were actually excavated, or more than three times the contractual minimum. The least
coverage was 0.6 tests per acre at the Cove and Sunset Ridge parcels at Lake Sam Rayburn (still
twice the minimum), and the greatest coverage was 1.9 tests per acre at Big Creek at Lake
Wright Patman (more than six times the minimum). Coverage at White Oak Creek Mitigation
Area consisted of 4.2 tests per acre. For the project as a whole, final coverage averaged 1.1 tests
per acre. This is equivalent to one test per 0.37 hectare or one test per 60 by 60 m grid square.

4.0 RESULTS
4.1 LAKE O’ THE PINES
The three parcels at Lake O’ the Pines totaled 364 acres and were 100% inventoried using 318
shovel tests. Only two of these tests were positive. These tests contained single, isolated lithic
flakes that were not clearly associated with any site. No cultural resource sites are present within
the Lake O’ the Pines parcels.
4.1.1 Highway 259
No cultural resource sites were present in the Highway 259 area. This 81 acre parcel was
completely inventoried using 115 shovel tests for a final density of 1.4 tests per acre. Of the 115
tests, 113 were negative. Two isolated artifacts were found in two different tests, but neither of
these was clearly associated with a site. The locations of shovel tests are plotted in Appendix A.
Table 4-1 Depth of Clay Deposits,

Typical soil profiles in the Highway 259 area were 10 to
20 cm of gray sandy loam over another 20 to 40 cm of
light brown loamy sand over either reddish brown clay
loam or strong brown clay. Twenty-two if the 115 tests
(19%) were terminated because of saturation. Of the 93
tests that did not encounter the water table, only 14 tests
(15%) still showed sand at 80 cm; the remaining 79 tests
(85%) reached clay deposits at depths less than 80 cm
(Table 4-1). The average depth of clay deposits was 51
cm below surface.

Isolated Artifacts
A single lithic flake was recovered from the upper 20 cm of test B-17 but no other cultural item
was found in this test or from other shovel tests in the surrounding area. As a result, this find
was designated an isolated artifact and is not considered a cultural resource site.
One lithic flake was recovered between 20 and 30 cm below the surface of test JH-8 but no other
cultural item was found in this test or from other shovel tests in the surrounding area. As a
result, this find was designated an isolated artifact and is not considered a cultural resource site.
4.1.2 Johnson Creek
No cultural resource sites were present in either of the two Johnson Creek sub-parcels. The 9acre eastern parcel was inventoried with 10 shovel tests for a final density of 1.1 tests per acre.
All tests were negative and no cultural material was found in this area. The 69 acres parcel on
the west was completed using 44 shovel tests for a final density of 0.6 shovel tests per acre. All
of these tests were similarly negative and no cultural material was found in this area. The
locations of shovel tests are plotted in Appendix A.

Soils in the Johnson Creek areas were shallow, typically
consisting of 10 cm of gray sandy loam over 20 to 50 cm
of mottled, yellowish-brown sandy loam over yellowishred loamy clay or hard-packed clay. Four shovel tests
contained gravels. None of the tests were inundated, and
all of the tests encountered clay deposits above 80 cm
(Table 4-2). One test (CK-7) reached sandstone bedrock
at 15 cm below surface. The average depth of clay
deposits was at 44 cm.

4.1.3 Ferrell’s Dam
No cultural resource sites were present in any of the three Ferrell’s Dam sub-parcels. The three
sub-areas totaled 205 acres and were completely inventoried using 181 shovel tests. Of these,
the pre-selected locations of eight tests were inundated and could not be tested and an additional
24 tests were not excavated due to steep slopes, ditches, or other surface disturbances. As a
result, 149 shovel tests were actually excavated for a final density of 0.7 shovel tests per acre.
All 149 of the excavated tests were negative and no cultural material was present in any test.
The locations of shovel tests are plotted in Appendix A.
Soil profiles in the Ferrell’s Dam area were highly
variable and no consistent profile could be determined.
In addition to the tests that were not excavated due to
various disturbances (see above), fully 53 of the
excavated tests (36%) showed subsurface evidence of
disturbance including 42 tests with soil mottling and
another 11 tests which contained gravels, concretions, or
bedrock. This evidence of disturbance was widespread
and is likely the result of extensive earth-moving
activities in the area immediately below the structure.
The average depth of clay deposits was 40 cm, with 23 of
the tests (15%) encountering clay deposits above 20 cm
(Table 4-3). Only seven of the tests (5%) could be
excavated to 80 cm or deeper but several of these still showed loamy sand to 100 cm.

Site 41MR108
Site 41MR108 was originally recorded on December 20, 1993 by Bo Nelson of the Northeast
Texas Archeological Society. It was described as a locus of prehistoric lithics exposed in a
eroded area immediately north of Big Cypress Creek. No temporal diagnostics were observed
and no features were noted. Site dimensions are not given, but the sketch map suggests that the
site is small and localized. The site is located immediately south of the extreme southern edge of
Ferrell’s Dam sub-parcel C. Shovel testing within sub-parcel C immediately north of the site
location found no evidence of the site. Because there is no evidence that the site extends within
the sub-parcel C, the proposed undertaking will not affect this site.

4.2 LAKE WRIGHT PATMAN
The four parcels at Lake Wright Patman totaled 546 acres and were 100% inventoried using 711
shovel tests; final testing density was 1.3 tests per acre. These tests confirmed the location of
one previously known site in the Highway 59 East parcel and recorded another newly discovered
site in the County Road Parcel. A second previously recorded site within the Highway 59 East
parcel could not be found despite intensive shovel testing in the location on record.
4.2.1 Big Creek
No cultural resource sites were present in the Big Creek parcel. This area totaled 67 acres and
was completely inventoried using 124 shovel tests for a final density of 1.9 shovel tests per acre.
All 124 of the tests were negative and no cultural material was present in any test. The locations
of shovel tests are plotted in Appendix A.

Soil profiles in the Big Creek area were shallow and
fairly uniform. The typical soil profile consisted of 20
to 35 cm of yellowish brown loamy sand over strong
brown clayey sand or clay loam. All of the 124 tests
encountered clay deposits above 80 cm, and 46 of the
tests (37%) encountered clay in the upper 20 cm (Table
4-4). For all tests, the average depth of clay was only
25 cm below the surface. Seventeen tests (14%) were
terminated above clay deposits because of saturation or
inundation and 22 tests (18%) showed mottled profiles.

shovel test density of 1.5 tests per acre. Of these tests, 60
were negative (92%) and the five positive tests defined site
41BW690. The locations of shovel tests are plotted in

Site 41BW690
This previously unknown site consists of a small concentration of buried prehistoric lithics. The
site is located on a small natural mound at an elevation of 250 ft AMSL. The aluminum site
marker was labeled with field number “11.” As indicated by positive shovel tests, the site
measures about 40 m by 25 m and has a total area of at approximately 0.25 acre. Artifacts were
found to 70 cm below the current ground surface.
Twelve shovel tests were dug to define this site. Soils were slightly deeper on this site than in
the surrounding area. One test encountered clay at 75 cm beneath the surface and the average
depth of clay was at 51 cm. The typical sub-surface profile consisted of 35 to 40 cm of gray
sandy loam over gray clayey sand grading to sandy clay. Of the 12 tests, five were positive and
yielded a total of 23 artifacts lithics. Artifact pervasiveness was 42% and density was 4.6
artifacts per positive test. All artifacts were lithic; no ceramics or ground items were present.
All but one of the lithics was small (< 2 cm) tertiary flakes of silicified wood. The single
exception was a tested siltstone cobble measuring 4.2 x 3.6 x 2.7 cm. Artifacts were buried to 70
cm beneath the ground surface, with a strong tendency towards the upper levels. Most artifacts
(65%) were found in the upper 40 cm (Table 4-6). No features were encountered. A sketch map
is presented in Figure 4-1.
Table 4-6 Artifact Frequency by Depth, Site 41BW690.

Soil profiles were fairly consistent on the site, consisting typically of 40 to 55 cm of massive
brown to yellow-brown loamy sand above strong brown sandy clay or mottled yellow-brown
mottled clay. The depth of clay on the site ranged from 31 to 75 cm and averaged 51 cm below
ground surface.
Because there is no stratigraphic or artifactual evidence of multiple occupations, it is assumed
that the assemblage represents a single occupation that has been vertically dispersed through the
massive sand by means of bioturbation. It is uncertain whether the site retains spatial integrity.
No ceramics, bone, or ground items were recovered and no features were observed. Nonetheless,
mound landforms are highly suggestive of habitation sites and subsurface features; additional
artifact types may be present. Because of the mound landform and because of the high
pervasiveness and density of artifacts, the site is therefore considered to have an unknown
potential for further research. The site may contain significant data sets but this has not been
clearly demonstrated through the limited shovel testing tactics. Until such time as additional
testing can be conducted to clearly ascertain NRHP eligibility, the site should be protected from
adverse impacts.

Soils in this area were extremely shallow, with clay
deposits generally found in the upper 20 cm. The typical
soil profile was 10 to 20 cm of brown or dark gray sandy
loam over reddish-yellow clay. Fully 314 of the tests
(93%) encountered clay above 40 cm and only a single

(Table 4-7). Twenty-three tests (7%) were terminated

profiles and only two tests contained gravels.

Isolated Artifacts
A single lithic flake was recovered from the upper 10 cm of test JH-6 but no other cultural item
was found in this test or from other shovel tests in the immediately surrounding area. As a result,
this find was designated an isolated artifact and is not considered a cultural resource site.
One lithic flake was recovered from the upper 10 cm of test JH-8 but no other cultural item was
found in this test or from other shovel tests in the immediately surrounding area. As a result, this
find was designated an isolated artifact and is not considered a cultural resource site.
Test E-1 yielded a metal strip measuring 3 by 22 cm from the upper 10 cm of deposits, but this
item appeared to be recent trash and was not considered further.
4.2.4 Highway 59 East
The Highway 59 East area totaled 177 acres and was completely inventoried using 186 shovel
tests for a final density of 1.1 tests per acre. Two cultural resource sites were present. Newly
discovered site 41BW691 was documented, and previously recorded site 41BW76 was
confirmed and documented. However, previously recorded site 41BW252 could not be found
despite extensive shovel testing in the recorded location. In addition, one shovel test recovered
six shards of modern window glass. These items were 2.0 to 2.4 mm thick, very slightly bluishgreen in hue, and appeared to be recent. They were recovered but were not considered further.
The locations of shovel tests are plotted in Appendix A.
Site 41BW691
This previously unknown site is a small, sparse concentration of prehistoric lithics located on a
terrace above an unnamed tributary to Elliot Creek. The site is at an elevation of 225 ft AMSL.
The aluminum site marker was labeled with field number “12.” As indicated by positive shovel
tests, the site measures about 30 by 20 m and has an area of less than 0.2 acres. Artifacts were
restricted to the upper 30 cm below the current ground surface.

Ecological Communications Corporation

Page 25

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

Eleven shovel tests were dug to define this site. All tests were fairly shallow. The average depth
to clay deposits on this site was less than 20 cm and only one test (ST-2) was dug to 40 cm
before encountering clay. The subsurface soil profile was typically 10 to 20 cm of gray to light
brown sandy loam over strong brown clay. Three of the 11 tests were positive for a 27%
pervasiveness measure. These yielded a total of nine lithics (Table 4-8). No ceramics, bone, or
ground artifacts were recovered. Artifact density was three artifacts per positive test. The lithics
were exclusively silicified wood, and all are small (< 2 cm) tertiary flakes. No features were
encountered. A sketch map is presented in Figure 4-2.

Based on the small size of the site, its shallowness, and its limited artifact diversity, the site is

Site 41BW76
This site was originally recorded on March 2, 1970 by James M. Malone during the inventory of
the “enlargement” to Lake Wright Patman, then known as Texarkana Reservoir. The site was
described as a large rise, “perhaps mound”, located in a partly tree-covered alluvial plain in the
bottom lands of Elliot Creek. Artifacts were observed eroding out of bank dirt and included
flakes, cores, plain and decorated ceramic sherds, bone and shell. Some potholes were observes
and the site was thought to be about 1 m deep.
Elevation of the site is 220 ft AMSL. As indicated by
positive shovel tests, the site measures about 125 m in
diameter and has an area of at approximately 4 acres (1.5
hectares). Artifacts were found to 70 cm below the current
ground surface. Twenty-one shovel tests were dug to
define this site. On the natural mound, soils were slightly
deeper than in the surrounding area, and the frequency
distribution of clay depth was strongly bimodal (Table 49). However, only one test did not encounter clay above
80 cm. Of the 21 shovel tests, 11 were positive and 10
negative for a pervasiveness measure of 52%. No features
were observed on the surface or in any of the tests.

The assemblage is fairly substantial for a shovel tested site and contains a remarkable diversity
of artifacts, including 28 lithics, 37 ceramic sherds, 13 bone fragments, and two shell hinges.
The sherds, though mostly small, include bone-tempered, sand-tempered, as well as grogtempered ceramics. Most of the sherds are plain wares but several incised sherds are also
present. The lithic assemblage consists of small (< 2 cm) tertiary flakes and a few pieces of
angular debris/shatter. No lithic tools or ground items were present. Lithic materials are mostly
silicified wood but include several specimens of quartzite, jasper, and chert as well. The bone
fragments are all small (< 1 cm) and unidentifiable and include both burned and unburned. One
canine tooth (possibly raccoon) and two hinges from freshwater bivalves were also present.
On the basis of the diversity and density of the artifact assemblage, coupled with the restricted
vertical distribution of artifacts, the site is assessed as containing excellent research potential.
The site should be protected from adverse impacts.
Site 41BW252
Site 41BW252 was originally reported in 1973 by Bill Young. The site was described as
consisting of two separate loci (Y41-BO-1, Y41-BO-2) situated about 30 m apart on small rises
on the floodplain north of the Sulphur River. The sites were separately plotted east of Highway
59 at 150 m north and 180 m north of the bridge at Elliot Creek. On the TARL maps the sites
are plotted as a single locus measuring approximately 250 m along a north-south axis by about
150 m along the east-west axis. Artifacts included bone tempered ceramics, dart points, and
bifaces. Young reported that in 1973 both sites were completely destroyed during highway
widening and that “nothing (is) left for future work” (Young 1973).
During the current project, this area was subjected to extensive shovel testing but no evidence of
the site could be found. Within the suspected site area, five inventory transects designated “A”
through “E” were established and shovel testing at 30 to 40 m intervals along these transects
resulted in approximately 20 tests within the suspected site area. No cultural material was found
in any test and the daily field log states “Site 41BW252 is supposed to be here but all of our tests

4.3 LAKE SAM RAYBURN
4.3.1 Marion’s Ferry
Three cultural resource sites are present within this 133 acre parcel. The inventory placed 236
shovel tests for a final shovel test density of 1.8 tests per acre, or more than 5 times the
contractual minimum of 0.33 tests per acre. Two previously unknown prehistoric sites and one
historic site were discovered and documented. The locations of shovel tests are plotted in
Appendix A.
Site 41AG184
Prior to fieldwork, the historic townsite of Marion was suspected to be located within the
Marion’s Ferry parcel. The USGS topographic map (Platt 7.5’) shows a symbol labeled “Site of
Marion” plotted on the bluff overlooking the Angelina River, adjacent to the road which
cross(ed) the river at this point and about 450’ (136 m) from the river thalweg. However, a
records search at TARL revealed that this location had not previously been recorded as an
archeological site.
This general location had been used by the
Spanish as a suitable crossing of the
Angelina River while traveling the LibertyNacogdoches Road (Martin 2002). The road
may have been used sporadically by local
inhabitants after the Spanish missions were
abandoned in 1773, but the area was not
settled until the early nineteenth century,
when a town known as McNeil’s Landing
was formed (Bowman 1976). Other names
for the town included Moses’ Bluff, Old
Marion’s Ferry, and finally Marion
(McMullen n.d.; Figure 4-4). In addition to
being an ideal spot for a ferry across the
river, the main attraction of the area was the
steamboat traffic that plied the Angelina
River loaded with dry goods and cotton.
Regular steamboat service ran from 1855
until 1882. During the 1830s and 1840s the
town prospered and in 1846 it became the
first county seat of Angelina County
(Haltom 1888).
At that time, Marion
consisted of frame buildings including a
drygoods store, a drug store, blacksmith
shop, a few homes, and the ferry landing
(Bowman 1976). A two-story courthouse
was later constructed and a post office was

Page 30

Figure 4-4 Marion Centennial Marker.
“Site of the town of Marion. Known as early as 1828
as McNeill’s Landing. In 1831 it consisted of over 200
buildings. First county seat of Angelina County. 18461854. Erected by the State of Texas 1936.”

Ecological Communications Corporation

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

established in 1847. However, for political reasons, the county seat was moved in 1854 to
Jonesville and the town began to decline. The final blow came in 1882 when the steamboat
stopped service after the railroad came through Angelina County. By 1899 the town was
abandoned. In 1936, a State of Texas marker was erected on the crest of the knoll (see Figure 44). The marker is inscribed:
Site of the town of Marion. Known as early as 1828 as McNeill’s
Landing. In 1831 it consisted of over 200 buildings. First county seat of
Angelina County. 1846-1854. Erected by the State of Texas 1936.
Before fieldwork, the precise location of the settlement of Marion was unknown. Logic
suggested that the town - built to service a ferry and the steamboat - would be near the river, but
the location and size of the settlement was unknown. It was also recognized that the stone
marker may or may not accurately mark the position of the town. Accordingly, the entire knoll
was systematically shovel

Soils on the crest of the knoll were shallow, with clay deposits generally found in the upper 10
cm. Along the downslope margins of the landform the soil profiles were somewhat deeper, but
only two of the 77 tests encountered clay below 60 cm and none went to 80 cm. The great
majority of tests (86%) encountered clay above 40 cm and the average depth of clay deposits on
the site was 25 cm below the surface. The typical soil profile was several cm of organic humus
over 20 cm of orange or yellow-brown sandy loam over red or orange-brown clay. Soils on the
site were thin but largely intact, with few tests noting mottling or gravels. No test encountered
standing water or saturation.
Today, the area is forested with second
growth pine trees, and a thick layer of
organic duff obscures the ground surface.
The few surface indications of the historic
site included some scattered bricks and
several concrete features. These features
include remnants of a concrete well
located at the extreme northern edge of the
parcel (Figures 4-6 and 4-7) and a
collapsed concrete trench.
This trench is located in the southwestern
portion of the site, approximately 50 m
northwest of the 1936 granite marker. The
trench is constructed of parallel slabs of
concrete and is approximately 9 m long.
The trench is interpreted as a cattle
dipping trough. The side slabs are about
18 to 20 cm in thickness and are 92 cm (36
inches) apart, measured at the northern end
where a corner remains intact. Elsewhere,
little structural integrity remains to the
feature. The west side is broken into
several large slabs that remain in general
alignment, but the eastern side has
completely collapsed into the trench to
form a depression about 2 m wide (Figure
4-8). The southern end is completely
collapsed. Numerous small fragments of
concrete rubble are dispersed around the
depression. About 7 m west of the trench
is a low mound, about 7 m long by 2.5 m
wide by about 70 cm high. The mound is
oriented parallel to the trench but is of
uncertain origin or association (Figure 49).
No artifacts were observed in
association with the trench or the mound.

All artifacts from shovel tests on Site 41AG184 were recovered from the upper 20 cm below the
surface, and some brick fragments were found in the upper 5 cm. A total of only 24 artifacts
were recovered. This assemblage consisted of 12 historic glass shards (including six clear, three
amber, two white, one purple), seven brick fragments, two ceramic sherds, two square nails, and

Ecological Communications Corporation

Page 33

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

one lead ball (Table 4-11). Brick fragments from test N-9 show extreme variation in paste with
random inclusions and appear to be hand made from local clays. Elsewhere however, stamped
brick fragments were present suggesting a mix of manufactured and hand-made (Figure 4-10).

The two ceramics are both very small white,
lead-glazed earthenware sherds. The sherd
from test R-7 measures 2.0 cm by 2.4 cm and
is 6 mm thick. It is undecorated and has
curvature in both dimensions. The sherd
from test R-7 measures 0.9 cm by 1.7 cm and
is 3 mm thick. It is decorated with a handpainted blue design element. This decoration
is on the interior of a slight curvature and the
topology suggests it may be from a saucer or
a cup.
The lead ball from test S-4 is slightly
flattened at 1.3 by 0.9 cm and appears to be a
fired projectile. While the manufacture of
lead balls was largely superceded by conical
lead projectiles by 1855, firearms are a highly
Figure 4-10 Bricks from Surface, Site
curated technology, and significant lag times
41AG184.
may occur before an older technology is
completely replaced; for this reason, the lead
ball may actually date up to several decades after 1855.
The two nails are both cut. Highly oxidized, they measure about 2.5 by 4.0 mm square, and are
broken at 4.5 cm and 5.1 cm in length, respectively. Wire nails were introduced after 1887,
suggesting that the site dates prior to that time.
None of the glass shards appear recent; all have surface scratches and wear consistent with long
burial. Both of the clear glass shards from test M-4 are 4 mm thick; the larger measures 2.6 cm
by 2.6 cm, has a molded lip, and appears to be from a drinking cup or glass. The solarized
purple glass shard measures 2.7 mm by 3.5 mm and is 6.5 mm thick and appears to be from a
molded bottle. Solarized purple glass is most common between 1880 and 1917.

Page 34

Ecological Communications Corporation

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

Finally, the glass shard from test O-8 measures 1.6 cm by 2.7 cm and is flat with no curvature.
The shard appears to be window glass. However, this shard is 1.5 mm thick at one end and is 3.0
mm thick at the opposite end, and this degree of thickness variation is extreme for such a small
fragment. The thickness of window glass has sometimes been used as a chronological indicator
on historic sites, but this single specimen can not help date the site.
The distribution of positive shovel tests covers an area about 400 m in diameter, or
approximately 16 hectares (40 acres). Although the density of historic artifacts is very sparse,
the entire distribution of artifacts is used to define the area of the site (see Figures 4-5, 4-7 and 49). Features are situated at the northern edge of the bluff, overlooking the river, and this may
have been the location of structures.
The low density of positive shovel tests and the small number of recovered artifacts was
surprising and problematic for a suspected historic town site occupied for more than 60 years and
reported to have contained 200 buildings. Accordingly, additional archival research was
undertaken after fieldwork and revealed that, despite the State of Texas marker on the site and
despite the USGS map symbol labeled “Town of Marion,” this knoll was in fact not the location
of the historic townsite. Rather, the town of Marion was apparently located at the bend of the
river west of the knoll, and is not located within the current inventory parcel (Figure 4-11). The
knoll top was instead the location of several farmsteads, including the P.N. Gilbert, Henry Hunt,
J.H. Ellison, and N.W. Gann survey plats. The interpretation of a rural farmstead(s) is more
consistent with the density and distribution of the historic artifacts on this site.
The historic site within the Marion’s ferry survey unit is clearly not the historically important
commercial townsite that was originally suspected. Rather, the site that is on the knoll top
contains the scattered remains of a historic farmstead. The site has a diversity of historic
artifacts including domestic and construction materials but these are thinly dispersed over a wide
area in extremely low numbers. Several historic concrete features are present, including a cattle
dipping trough and a well casing, but these are in poor condition and have no remaining
structural integrity. As a result, the site has very limited potential for further archeological
research. The site may have some limited potential for further archival research. However, on
the basis of the shovel testing tactics applied during this field visit, the site is assessed as not
eligible for inclusion to the National Register of Historic Places. No further management is
recommended.
Site 41AG182
This previously unknown site is a concentration of prehistoric lithics and ceramics located on a
low knoll just south of the Angelina River. The site is at an elevation of 170 ft AMSL. The
aluminum site marker was labeled with field number “05.” As indicated by positive shovel tests,
the site measures about 40 m by 60 m and has an area of at approximately 0.5 acre. Artifacts
extended to 80 cm below the current ground surface.
Twenty-three tests were dug to define this site. All tests were excavated to at least 95 cm and the
deepest test was dug to 110 cm. All tests contained uniform, massive orange-brown sand; no test
contained any clay deposits. Nine of the 23 tests were positive. These yielded 28 artifacts

Vertical distribution of artifacts ranged broadly between 0 and 80 cm but shows a pronounced
central tendency at 40 to 60 cm (see Table 4-12). Nearly half of all artifacts (46%) are restricted
to a single level (40 to 60 cm) and 71% are restricted to two levels (40 to 80 cm).
Although the presumably single occupation has been vertically dispersed through the massive
sand deposits, the concentration of artifacts at 40 to 60 cm strongly suggests that the site may
retain some good spatial integrity. It is considered possible that the site may have potential for
further research. However, on the basis of the limited shovel testing tactics applied during this
field visit it is unclear whether or not this site may be eligible for inclusion to the NRHP. Until
such time as additional testing can be conducted to clearly ascertain such eligibility, the site
should be protected from adverse impacts.
Site 41AG183
This previously unknown site is a concentration of prehistoric lithics and ceramics located on a
low knoll just south of the Angelina River. The site is at an elevation of 165 ft AMSL. The
aluminum site marker was labeled with field number “10.” As indicated by positive shovel tests,
the site measures about 40 m by 10 m and has an area of less than 0.25 acre. Artifacts extended
to 80 cm below the current ground surface.
Twelve tests were dug to define this site. All tests but one were excavated to 80 cm or deeper
and four tests still showed sand at 95 cm. The sub-surface profile was uniform, massive orangebrown sand; only one test (ST-9) contained clay at 60 cm. Of the 12 tests, eight were positive
and yielded a total of 39 artifacts including 33 lithics and six ceramics. These are fairly high
levels of pervasiveness (67% of tests are positive) and density (4.9 artifacts per positive test).

assemblage. Only one lithic item was larger than 2 cm; this was a piece of angular debris/shatter

Vertical distribution of artifacts was extremely broad between the surface and 80 cm and there

degree of vertical mixing strongly suggests horizontal mixing as well, since bioturbation tends to
proceed in all directions, not just up-and-down. Although overall artifact density is high, it is
unlikely that the site retains much spacial integrity. In addition, no features were encountered.
The site is therefore considered to have a low potential for further research and is recommended
as not eligible for inclusion to the NRHP. No further management is recommended.

4.3.2 Jackson Park
No cultural resource sites were present in the Jackson Park area. This 438 acre parcel was
completely inventoried using 531 shovel tests for a final density of 1.2 shovel tests per acre. In
addition to these 531 excavated tests, the pre-selected locations of four tests could not be
excavated due to steep slope. All tests were negative. No cultural material was found in any of
the 531 tests. The locations of shovel tests are plotted in Appendix A.

Site 41JP151
This previously unknown site consists of shallowly buried prehistoric lithics and is located on a
high terrace slope northeast of Squirrel Creek, a minor tributary to the Angelina River. The site
is at an elevation of 180 ft AMSL. The aluminum site marker was labeled with field number
“06.” As indicated by positive shovel tests, the site measures about 20 m by 26 m and has an
area of at approximately 0.1 acre. Artifacts were found to 40 cm below the current ground
surface.
Six shovel tests were dug to define this site. One test encountered clay at 75 cm beneath the
surface but all others reached clay between 35 and 40 cm. The typical sub-surface profile
consisted of 35 to 40 cm of gray sandy loam over gray clayey sand grading to sandy clay. Of the
six tests, only two were positive and yielded a total of seven lithics. No ceramics or ground
items were present. The lithics were all small (< 2 cm) tertiary flakes of silicified wood. All
artifacts were shallowly buried in the upper 40 cm (Table 4-16) with a negligible mode in the
upper 20 cm. No features were encountered. A sketch map for site 41JP151 is presented in
Figure 4-14.

4.4 STEINHAGEN LAKE
4.4.1 Spring Creek East
The single parcel at Steinhagen Lake totaled 178 acres and was 100% inventoried using 120
shovel tests for a net density of 0.7 tests per acre. Two previously unknown sites were found and
recorded. In addition, two tests contained single, isolated lithic flakes that were not clearly
associated with any site. The locations of shovel tests are plotted in Appendix A.
Site 41JP153
This previously unknown site consists of shallowly buried prehistoric lithics and ceramics and is
located on a small natural mound in the Kelly Branch flood plain, a tributary to the Angelina
River. The site is at an elevation of 88 ft AMSL. The aluminum site marker was labeled with
field number “18.” As indicated by positive shovel tests, the site measures about 40 m by 30 m
and has an area of about 0.25 acre. Artifacts are found to 40 cm below the ground surface.
Eight shovel tests were dug to define this site. The subsurface
profile was highly variable. Four tests encountered clay at
depths below 55 cm while clay was found at 15 cm or less in
two other tests (Table 4-17). The upper horizon was strong
brown silty sand or loamy sand over strong brown clay or
mottled clayey sand. Of the eight tests, three were positive and
yielded a total of 13 items including 10 lithics and three
ceramics. No ground items were present and no features were
encountered. Most of the lithics were all small (< 2 cm)
tertiary flakes of silicified wood or other silicates. The largest
lithic was a laminar piece of silicified wood measuring 4.5 x 3 x 1 cm that has one edge which
appeared to have been used as an expedient cutting tool. The three ceramics were all found in
the same test (ST-1). They were fairly large (>5 cm) and undecorated with sand temper and
appear to be from the same vessel. All artifacts were shallowly buried in the upper 40 cm with a
strong mode between 20 and 40 cm (Table 4-18). A sketch map is presented in Figure 4-15.

Site 41JP152
This previously unknown site consists of shallowly buried prehistoric lithics and ceramics and is
located on the first terrace above the Kelly Branch flood plain, a tributary to the Angelina River.
The site is at an elevation of 93 ft AMSL. The aluminum site marker was labeled with field
number “20.” As indicated by positive shovel tests, the site measures about 145 m (N-S) by

Page 44

Ecological Communications Corporation

Cultural Resource Inventories of Selected Areas at Four Lakes in East Texas

about 55 m (E-W). The site has an area of about 2 acres. Artifacts are found to 60 cm below the
current ground surface.
Twenty-four shovel tests were dug to define the limits of
this site. Soil profiles were somewhat deeper than elsewhere
in the inventory parcel and none of the tests found clay at
depths shallower than 20 cm. The average depth of clay
deposits on the site was at 49 cm and eight tests found clay
between 60 and 80 cm (Table 4-19). In general, the upper
stratum was yellow-brown sand or sandy loam over yellowbrown hard-packed sandy clay. While artifacts were found
in the 50 to 60 cm level, the majority (89%) were found in
the upper 40 cm, with a strong mode between 20 and 40 cm
(Table 4-20). This fairly restricted vertical distribution suggests that the site may retain good
spatial integrity. Of the 24 tests, 14 were positive for a pervasiveness measure of 56%. These
positive tests yielded a total of 53 artifacts for a net artifact density of 3.8 items per
positive test. The assemblage consists of 36 lithics, 15 ceramic sherds, and two bone fragments.
No ground items were present and no features were encountered. Most of the lithics were very
small (< 2 cm) tertiary flakes. Several small pieces of angular debris/shatter were also present. A
variety of crypto-crystalline silicates was represented, but silicified wood was dominant. A
sketch map is presented in Figure 4-16.

Two projectile points were included in this assemblage. A complete point resembling a Gary

suggesting the tool was manufactured from a small nodule. The point measures 4.7 cm long by

the fine flaking that is characteristic of Perdiz points. The fragment
consist of the stem and one barb and measures 1.9 cm long by slightly

have measured about 3 cm long by about 1.6 cm wide. The two
projectile points suggest a possible multi-component occupation of
this location. The Gary point is characteristic of Archaic sites while
the Perdiz point is diagnostic of the Late Prehistoric Period.
Alternatively, the earlier Gary point may simply have been curated
onto the later site.
Most of the ceramics are sand tempered plain ware but two sherds
contained some bone temper. Three of the sand tempered sherds were
fairly large, measuring 4 to 6 cm in size. Both of the recovered bone
fragments were very small (< 2 cm), burned, and unidentifiable. No
shell was recovered.
About 80 m south of the southern edge of the site, shovel test WA38 recovered one lithic from the upper 20 cm and another six lithics
and a small ceramic sherd between 20 and 40 cm. No other cultural
items were recovered from other shovel tests in the immediately
surrounding area and this find was classified in the field as isolated.
However, based on the number of artifacts recovered from this one
test together with its relative proximity to site 41JP152, it is possible
that these artifacts are related to site 41JP152. If so, then the site may
be larger than here delineated.
